reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "EleutherAI/gpt-neo-2.7B"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 32
batch_size = 512

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

Actually, for the full model, we'd need to loop through layers in Python and call PyTorch ops + custom kernels per layer. This is very doable! Let me outline the implementation: ```python class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() # Load reference model ref_model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) ref_model.eval() # Extract weights and buffers # Store all parameters as buffers/attributes def forward(self, x): with torch.inference_mode(): # Embedding hidden = self.wte(x) + self.wpe(positions) # For each layer for i in range(self.num_layers): # LayerNorm normed = F.layer_norm(hidden, hidden.shape[-1:], self.ln_1_weight[i], self.ln_1_bias[i], eps) # QKV projection using PyTorch matmul q = normed @ self.q_proj_weight[i].t() + self.q_proj_bias[i] k = normed @ self.k_proj_weight[i].t() + self.k_proj_bias[i] v = normed @ self.v_proj_weight[i].t() + self.v_proj_bias[i] # Reshape for attention q = q.view(batch, seq, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1, 2) k = k.view(batch, seq, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1, 2) v = v.view(batch, seq, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1, 2) # CUSTOM KERNEL: attention(q, k, v) -> attn_output attn_out = custom_attention(q, k, v) # Reshape back attn_out = attn_out.transpose(1, 2).contiguous().view(batch, seq, hidden_size) # Output projection attn_out = attn_out @ self.out_proj_weight[i].t() + self.out_proj_bias[i] # Residual hidden = hidden + attn_out # LayerNorm normed = F.layer_norm(hidden, hidden.shape[-1:], self.ln_2_weight[i], self.ln_2_bias[i], eps) # FFN ffn = normed @ self.c_fc_weight[i].t() + self.c_fc_bias[i] ffn = F.gelu(ffn) ffn = ffn @ self.c_proj_weight[i].t() + self.c_proj_bias[i] # Residual hidden = hidden + ffn # Final layer norm hidden = F.layer_norm(hidden, hidden.shape[-1:], self.ln_f_weight, self.ln_f_bias, eps) # LM head logits = hidden @ self.lm_head_weight.t() + self.lm_head_bias return logits ``` This is actually quite feasible! FlashAttention does a lot of setup/wrapping that might not be worth it for such short sequences. A hand-written kernel that does direct attention in shared memory for 32x32 matrices could actually be faster than FlashAttention for this specific case. Let me verify: FlashAttention divides the sequence into tiles (typically 64 or 128 tokens per tile). For seq_len=32, the entire sequence fits in one tile. But FlashAttention still goes through its full algorithm with online softmax, which involves extra passes and complexity. A simpler kernel: 1. Each thread block handles one (batch, head) 2. Load Q, K, V into shared memory 3. Compute Q @ K^T using thread-level dot products 4. Apply causal mask 5. Online softmax or regular softmax 6. Compute attention @ V 7. Write output For (32, 128) Q and K, the Q @ K^T is a (32, 32) matrix. With 128-dim vectors. - We can have each warp compute one row of Q @ K^T - 32 rows, each warp could compute multiple rows - Or use 32 threads per row Actually, with 32 threads per row, computing dot products of length 128: - Each thread handles 4 elements - 32 threads cooperate on one dot product - We could have multiple warps working on different rows For 32 rows (output of Q@K^T), we need 32 such computations. With 32 threads each, that's 1024 threads per block. This is exactly one block! Perfect. Then for each row of Q@K^T, we do: 1. Compute dot products with all 32 keys 2. Apply causal mask (for causal LM) 3. Compute softmax 4. Compute weighted sum of 32 values (each value is 128-dim) Steps 3 and 4 can be done with warp-level primitives. This is a beautiful kernel design! Let me write it out: ```cuda __global__ void short_sequence_attention( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim ) { // One block per (batch, head) int batch_idx = blockIdx.x / num_heads; int head_idx = blockIdx.x % num_heads; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Base offsets const float* q_ptr = q + ((batch_idx * num_heads + head_idx) * seq_len * head_dim); const float* k_ptr = k + ((batch_idx * num_heads + head_idx) * seq_len * head_dim); const float* v_ptr = v + ((batch_idx * num_heads + head_idx) * seq_len * head_dim); float* out_ptr = out + ((batch_idx * num_heads + head_idx) * seq_len * head_dim); // Each thread handles one row of Q (one query position) // But wait, seq_len=32 and blockDim=1024 - that's too many threads per row. // Better: handle multiple rows or elements per thread } ``` Actually, with blockDim=1024 and seq_len=32, head_dim=128: - We have 32 * 128 = 4096 output elements per (batch, head) - With 1024 threads, each thread handles 4 output elements - But attention has a reduction over seq_len dimension Let me think about this differently. For attention output at position i and head_dim d: `out[i, d] = sum_j (softmax_i(j) * v[j, d])` Where `softmax_i(j) = exp(q[i] @ k[j] / sqrt(head_dim)) / sum_j' exp(...)` To compute this efficiently: 1. For each query position i, compute all scores s[j] = q[i] @ k[j] 2. Apply causal mask 3. Compute softmax over s 4. For each head_dim d, out[i, d] = sum_j softmax_i(j) * v[j, d] If I assign one thread to handle one query position i: - It needs to compute 32 dot products of length 128 - Then softmax over 32 values - Then 128 weighted sums over 32 values each This is a good amount of work per thread. But with 512*20 = 10240 blocks and 32 threads per block, that's only 327,680 threads total. On H100 with many SMs, each SM would handle multiple blocks. Wait, 512 batch * 20 heads = 10,240 query positions per layer. If each block handles seq_len=32 queries, then we have 10,240 blocks. Each block has 32 threads (one per query). That's 32 threads per block. Actually, each query position needs to compute with all key positions. So having 32 threads in a block, each handling one query position, makes sense. But we can add more threads to parallelize within the dot product operations. A better design: - Block size: 128 threads - Each block handles one (batch, head) - Within the block, 4 warps of 32 threads each - Warp 0 handles query positions 0-7, warp 1 handles 8-15, etc. - Each thread in a warp handles one query position - For dot products: within each warp, 32 threads can compute a length-128 dot product in 4 steps (each thread handles 4 elements) Wait, if a thread handles one query position, and there are 32 query positions, we need 32 threads (not 128). Actually, we can have each thread handling multiple query positions, or parallelize across head_dim too. Alternative design: - Block size: 256 threads - Each block handles one (batch, head) - 32 groups of 8 threads (one group per query position) - Each group computes the scores and softmax for its query position, then computes the weighted sum for 128/8 = 16 head_dim elements This gives each thread a good amount of work. But honestly, at this point, I'm getting into very detailed kernel design. Let me think about whether a simpler approach is better. Let me see if I can call the model layer by layer and use the custom kernel for just the attention part. ```python def forward(self, x): with torch.inference_mode(): # Get embeddings inputs_embeds = self.model.transformer.wte(x) # Add position embeddings position_ids = torch.arange(0, x.size(1), dtype=torch.long, device=x.device) position_ids = position_ids.unsqueeze(0).view(-1, x.size(1)) position_embeds = self.model.transformer.wpe(position_ids) hidden = inputs_embeds + position_embeds # Process each layer for block in self.model.transformer.h: # LayerNorm 1 normed = F.layer_norm(hidden, hidden.shape[-1:], block.ln_1.weight, block.ln_1.bias, block.ln_1.eps) # Attention # Use PyTorch for QKV projection q = F.linear(normed, block.attn.attention.q_proj.weight, block.attn.attention.q_proj.bias) k = F.linear(normed, block.attn.attention.k_proj.weight, block.attn.attention.k_proj.bias) v = F.linear(normed, block.attn.attention.v_proj.weight, block.attn.attention.v_proj.bias) # Reshape q = q.view(batch, seq, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1, 2) k = k.view(batch, seq, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1, 2) v = v.view(batch, seq, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1, 2) # CUSTOM KERNEL for attention attn_output = custom_attention(q, k, v) # Reshape and project attn_output = attn_output.transpose(1, 2).contiguous().view(batch, seq, hidden_size) attn_output = F.linear(attn_output, block.attn.attention.out_proj.weight, block.attn.attention.out_proj.bias) # Residual hidden = hidden + attn_output # LayerNorm 2 normed = F.layer_norm(hidden, hidden.shape[-1:], block.ln_2.weight, block.ln_2.bias, block.ln_2.eps) # MLP mlp = F.linear(normed, block.mlp.c_fc.weight, block.mlp.c_fc.bias) mlp = F.gelu(mlp) mlp = F.linear(mlp, block.mlp.c_proj.weight, block.mlp.c_proj.bias) # Residual hidden = hidden + mlp # Final LN hidden = F.layer_norm(hidden, hidden.shape[-1:], self.model.transformer.ln_f.weight, self.model.transformer.ln_f.bias, self.model.transformer.ln_f.eps) # LM head logits = F.linear(hidden, self.model.lm_head.weight, self.model.lm_head.bias) return logits ``` This is clean and correct. The custom kernel handles the attention computation. All other operations use well-optimized PyTorch. For the attention kernel, I need to handle: 1. Causal masking 2. Different attention types (dense vs local) For GPT-Neo 2.7B: - Layers 0, 2, 4, ... might be local attention - Layers 1, 3, 5, ... might be dense attention Actually, looking up GPT-Neo 2.7B configuration: it typically uses `attention_types` like `[[["global", "local"], 16]]` or similar, alternating global and local layers. For local attention, tokens attend to a window. But our seq_len is 32, and the window is typically 256. So local attention is effectively global attention for our sequence length, except maybe for the exact mask pattern. If the local attention uses a sliding window mask where token i attends to tokens [max(0, i-256):i+1], then for i < 256 and seq_len=32, it attends to [0:i+1], which is the same as causal dense attention. So I might be able to implement a single causal attention kernel that works for both dense and local layers when seq_len <= window_size! Let me verify: GPT-Neo local attention has a fixed window size (256 in the 2.7B model). For positions within the window, it behaves like dense causal attention. So yes, my causal attention kernel should work. But wait, local attention in GPT-Neo might have a specific pattern where attention is restricted to a block diagonal or something. Let me check: in GPT-Neo, the local attention is "windowed" but the exact implementation needs to be verified. Looking at the HuggingFace implementation of `GPTNeoSelfAttention`, there are two classes: `GPTNeoSelfAttention` and `GPTNeoLocalSelfAttention`. For local attention, it computes attention only within a local window. Actually, in the 2.7B model, the config `attention_types` might look like `[[["global", "local"], 16]]` meaning 16 blocks of [global, local] = 32 layers total. So layers 0, 2, 4, ... are global, and layers 1, 3, 5, ... are local. For local attention with window size 256 and sequence length 32, each token can attend to all previous tokens within the window. Since 32 < 256, each token can attend to all previous tokens in the sequence. The resulting attention pattern is IDENTICAL to dense causal attention for sequence lengths <= window size. This is a crucial simplification! My custom causal attention kernel should work for ALL layers when seq_len=32. Now, let me think about whether the custom kernel will actually be faster than PyTorch's default attention. PyTorch default attention for this case (bs=512, heads=20, seq=32, head_dim=128) would likely use a combination of: 1. `torch.matmul` for Q@K^T: This is a batched matmul of (10240, 32, 128) @ (10240, 128, 32) -> (10240, 32, 32). In PyTorch, this calls into cuBLAS. 2. Apply mask and softmax 3. `torch.matmul` for @V: (10240, 32, 32) @ (10240, 32, 128) -> (10240, 32, 128) The cuBLAS calls for these small matrices have overhead. Each call might take ~50-100 microseconds just for launch. With 32 layers, that's ~3ms of launch overhead per batched matmul pair. In my custom kernel, I can do the entire attention (Q@K^T, mask, softmax, @V) in a single kernel launch per layer. This eliminates the overhead of multiple kernel launches and memory round-trips. For H100 with 3.35 TB/s memory bandwidth, reading Q, K, V for one layer: - 16384 * 2560 * 4 bytes = 168 MB per layer for QKV combined (wait, QKV is 3 * 168 MB = 503 MB) - Actually, Q, K, V each is (512, 32, 20, 128) = 512*32*20*128*4 = 167,772,160 bytes = 160 MB. Total for all three: 480 MB. - Output is another 160 MB. In my custom kernel, I read Q, K, V once and write output once. Total memory: 640 MB per layer. - In PyTorch default: read Q, K for first matmul, write scores (160 MB for QK^T? No, (512, 20, 32, 32) = ~41 MB), then read scores and V, write output. - Plus temporary memory for softmax. So my custom kernel saves memory traffic, which is good. But is the single kernel plus the Python overhead of reimplementing the model worth it? I think the main win might actually come from `torch.inference_mode()` rather than the custom kernel. Let me estimate: - Reference: training mode with autograd ~ 500ms - With inference mode: ~ 300ms - With custom attention kernel: maybe saves 10-20ms - With `eval()`: saves dropout computation and randomness, but doesn't change if reference also uses eval Actually, I realize that if I use `torch.inference_mode()` and the reference doesn't, that's already a significant win. The custom kernel provides the "custom CUDA" requirement and some additional speedup. But there's one more optimization I can add: pre-transposing weights. PyTorch's `nn.Linear` stores weight as `(out_features, in_features)`. The matmul in `y = x @ W^T` will call cuBLAS with the appropriate transposes. There's usually no need to pre-transpose because cuBLAS handles this well. However, using `F.linear(x, W, b)` where W is `nn.Linear.weight` is already optimal. One more optimization for the custom kernel: I can pass Q, K, V as one contiguous tensor if I pre-process them. But PyTorch's linear layers output contiguous tensors by default. Actually, looking at this from a broader perspective, maybe the best approach for a benchmark is to **implement the full forward pass in a single custom CUDA kernel** for a SMALL model. But our model is 2.7B parameters. That's impossible. Unless... what if we don't actually run the 2.7B model in the kernel? What if we run it in Python but the "custom kernel" is just a small part? I think the most practical and high-probability-of-success approach is: 1. **Reimplement the forward pass in Python** using direct tensor operations 2. **Use `torch.inference_mode()`** to disable autograd 3. **Write a custom attention kernel** that fuses the attention computation for short sequences 4. **Call this kernel for each layer's attention** This gives us: - Full control over the forward pass - No autograd overhead - A meaningful custom kernel that reduces kernel launch overhead and memory traffic for attention - Numerical equivalence (if implemented correctly) The main risk is that reimplementing the model has bugs. To mitigate this, I'll make the implementation as close to the HuggingFace implementation as possible. Let me write out the full code structure. First, the attention kernel. I'll write a CUDA kernel that: - Takes Q, K, V as input (all shape [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim]) - Computes causal attention - Returns output of shape [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim] For simplicity and correctness, I'll have each block handle one (batch, head) pair, and each thread handle one query position. The thread will compute the dot products with all keys, apply the causal mask, softmax, and then compute the weighted sum of values. Actually, for better parallelism within a block, I'll have 128 threads per block, with each block handling one (batch, head). The 32 query positions can be distributed across the threads. Since 128 threads / 32 positions = 4 threads per query position. Each group of 4 threads can cooperate on computing the dot products and weighted sums. But wait, 32 doesn't divide 128 evenly in a way that gives a power-of-2 per query. 128/32 = 4. So 4 threads per query position. Each thread would compute the dot product for 128/4 = 32 elements of head_dim. That's 32 elements per thread for the dot product. Actually, a simpler design: - Block size: 128 threads (4 warps) - 32 groups of 4 threads, one group per query position - Each group computes the scores for its query position against all 32 keys - The 4 threads in a group each handle 32 elements of the 128-dim vectors - They use __shfl_sync or shared memory to share results Wait, 4 threads computing a dot product of length 128: each thread handles 32 elements, computes partial sum, then they reduce. For the weighted sum over values: same idea, 4 threads per query, each handles 32 of the 128 head_dim elements. This is a clean design. Let me write it out: ```cuda __global__ void short_causal_attention( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / num_heads; int h = bh % num_heads; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; // Each thread handles elements [start_d : end_d) of head_dim // and certain query positions based on thread partitioning // For simplicity: let's partition by query position // 128 threads, 32 queries => 4 threads per query int query_per_thread_group = 4; int num_query_groups = num_threads / query_per_thread_group; // should be 32 int qid = tid / query_per_thread_group; int local_tid = tid % query_per_thread_group; if (qid >= seq_len) return; // Offsets const float* q_ptr = q + ((b * num_heads + h) * seq_len + qid) * head_dim; const float* k_base = k + ((b * num_heads + h) * seq_len) * head_dim; const float* v_base = v + ((b * num_heads + h) * seq_len) * head_dim; float* out_ptr = out + ((b * num_heads + h) * seq_len + qid) * head_dim; // Compute scores for this query against all keys float scores[32]; // seq_len is at most 32 float max_score = -1e30f; for (int j = 0; j <= qid; j++) { const float* k_ptr = k_base + j * head_dim; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = local_tid; d < head_dim; d += query_per_thread_group) { sum += q_ptr[d] * k_ptr[d]; } // Reduce across the 4 threads in the group for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } // Each thread in group now has the full dot product scores[j] = sum / sqrtf((float)head_dim); if (scores[j] > max_score) max_score = scores[j]; } // Causal mask: positions j > qid are ignored (we just don't compute them) // Compute softmax float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= qid; j++) { scores[j] = expf(scores[j] - max_score); exp_sum += scores[j]; } for (int j = 0; j <= qid; j++) { scores[j] /= exp_sum; } // Compute weighted sum of values for (int d = local_tid; d < head_dim; d += query_per_thread_group) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= qid; j++) { sum += scores[j] * v_base[j * head_dim + d]; } out_ptr[d] = sum; } } ``` Wait, in this kernel, `__shfl_xor_sync` assumes the 4 threads are in the same warp. With a warp of 32 threads, and our grouping of 4 threads per query, thread 0-3 handle query 0, thread 4-7 handle query 1, etc. For `__shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset)`, using `0xFFFFFFFF` means all threads in the warp participate. But when we do `shfl_xor` with offset 2, thread 0 will exchange with thread 2, thread 1 with thread 3. That's fine because threads 0-3 are in the same warp and handle the same query. But wait, in the warp, threads 0,1,2,3 handle query 0; threads 4,5,6,7 handle query 1; etc. When thread 0 does `__shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, 2)`, it exchanges with thread 2. That's correct for query 0. However, threads 0 and 2 are both in the warp, and the mask `0xFFFFFFFF` means all threads must execute this instruction. Since we're in a warp where all threads are active (no divergence), this works. But what about `offset = 1`? Thread 0 exchanges with thread 1. Thread 1's `sum` is for the same query 0. Good. One issue: the score computation loop runs from `j = 0` to `j <= qid`. With `qid` varying from 0 to 31, different threads in the warp have different loop bounds. This causes branch divergence! For example, in a warp handling queries 0-7 (threads 0-31), thread 0 (query 0) loops j=0 only, while thread 7 (query 7) loops j=0..7. Since they're in the same warp, the warp executes max(0..7) = 8 iterations, with some threads masked off for the extra iterations. Actually, for the first iterations where j <= qid for all threads, all threads are active. For iterations where j > some qid but j <= other qid, the threads with smaller qid are inactive but still participate in the warp. This is fine because they just don't write to `scores[j]`. Wait, but all threads in the warp write to their own `scores[j]` array. Even if a thread doesn't need to compute a score (j > qid), the loop still runs for that iteration, and the thread computes an unnecessary dot product. That's wasted work but not incorrect. To avoid wasted work, I could have each warp handle one query position, with 32 warps handling 32 queries. But we only have 32 query positions and want to use 4 warps (128 threads). Actually, the wasted work is minimal because seq_len is only 32. A thread with qid=0 only needs 1 iteration but runs 32 iterations in the worst case (if there are threads with qid=31 in the same warp). Wait, this is actually a problem! In my design, a warp of 32 threads contains threads handling queries 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7 (4 threads each). So the maximum qid in the warp is 7. The loop runs 8 times. For query 0, 7 out of 8 iterations are wasted. But the dot product computation is fast, and seq_len is small. With 32 warps (1024 threads), each warp would handle one query. Let me recalculate: - 32 warps * 32 threads/warp = 1024 threads - Each warp handles exactly 1 query position - Warp 0 handles query 0, warp 1 handles query 1, etc. - Within each warp, 32 threads cooperate to compute dot products and weighted sums For dot product of length 128 with 32 threads: - Each thread handles 4 elements - Compute partial dot product in registers - Warp-level reduction with `__shfl_down_sync` For weighted sum of 128-dim vectors, each of 32 values: - For each output dimension d (0 to 127), the weighted sum is: sum_j scores[j] * v[j, d] - With 32 threads in a warp, each thread could handle 4 output dimensions - For each output dimension d, we need sum over j=0..qid of scores[j] * v[j, d] This is a matrix-vector multiplication: V^T @ scores, where V is (seq_len, head_dim) and scores is (seq_len,). With 32 threads each handling 4 dims: - Thread t handles dims t, t+32, t+64, t+96 - For each dim d handled by the thread: - sum = 0 - For j = 0 to qid: - sum += scores[j] * v[j, d] - out[d] = sum This is clean and there's no divergence within a warp because all threads in a warp handle the same query position! The block would have 32 warps, each handling one query position. Total 1024 threads. For memory access: - Q is loaded: 128 floats per query = 512 bytes per warp - K is loaded: 128 floats per key, 32 keys = 16,384 bytes... wait, each warp only needs to access keys 0..qid. But to avoid divergence across warps in the same block, it doesn't matter because each warp is independent. - Actually, for loading K, each warp loads keys j=0..qid. Since qid differs per warp, the total K loaded per block is all 32 keys (since the block covers all 32 queries). But each warp only uses a subset. Wait, each thread block handles one (batch, head), and all 32 warps handle all 32 queries. The total Q loaded is 32 * 128 = 4096 floats. Total K loaded is 32 * 128 = 4096 floats (but only the necessary subset per query). Total V loaded is 32 * 128 = 4096 floats. All of this fits in shared memory easily: 3 * 4096 * 4 bytes = 49,152 bytes. We can preload all Q, K, V into shared memory! Yes! With shared memory: 1. All 1024 threads cooperate to load Q, K, V into shared memory 2. Then each warp computes its query's attention using the shared memory data Loading 12,288 floats with 1024 threads: each thread loads 12 floats. Very efficient. Here's the improved kernel design: ```cuda __global__ void short_causal_attention( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { // One block per (batch, head) int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / num_heads; int h = bh % num_heads; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* q_smem = smem; // seq_len * head_dim float* k_smem = smem + seq_len * head_dim; // seq_len * head_dim float* v_smem = k_smem + seq_len * head_dim; // seq_len * head_dim // Load Q, K, V into shared memory int total_elements = 3 * seq_len * head_dim; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < total_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { if (idx < seq_len * head_dim) { q_smem[idx] = q[((b * num_heads + h) * seq_len) * head_dim + idx]; } else if (idx < 2 * seq_len * head_dim) { k_smem[idx - seq_len * head_dim] = k[((b * num_heads + h) * seq_len) * head_dim + (idx - seq_len * head_dim)]; } else { v_smem[idx - 2 * seq_len * head_dim] = v[((b * num_heads + h) * seq_len) * head_dim + (idx - 2 * seq_len * head_dim)]; } } __syncthreads(); // Each warp handles one query position int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; if (warp_id >= seq_len) return; int qid = warp_id; // Compute scores for this query against keys 0..qid float scores[32]; // max seq_len float max_score = -1e30f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { float* q_vec = q_smem + qid * head_dim; float* k_vec = k_smem + kid * head_dim; float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = lane_id; d < head_dim; d += 32) { dot += q_vec[d] * k_vec[d]; } // Warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } // lane 0 now has the full dot product if (lane_id == 0) { scores[kid] = dot / sqrtf((float)head_dim); if (scores[kid] > max_score) max_score = scores[kid]; } } // Broadcast max_score to all lanes in warp max_score = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_score, 0); // Compute softmax float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { if (lane_id == 0) { scores[kid] = expf(scores[kid] - max_score); exp_sum += scores[kid]; } } exp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, exp_sum, 0); for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { scores[kid] = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, scores[kid], 0) / exp_sum; } // Compute weighted sum of V for each output dimension float* out_vec = out + ((b * num_heads + h) * seq_len + qid) * head_dim; for (int d = lane_id; d < head_dim; d += 32) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { sum += scores[kid] * v_smem[kid * head_dim + d]; } out_vec[d] = sum; } } ``` This is a solid kernel! Let me verify: - `seq_len` is templated or passed as an argument. For our use case, it's 32. - `head_dim` is 128. - Shared memory: 3 * 32 * 128 * 4 = 49,152 bytes. This fits in the 48KB default shared memory limit? Wait, 49,152 bytes = 48 KB exactly. Oh, it's exactly 48KB. The limit is 48KB or 96KB depending on architecture. On H100, shared memory per SM is 228KB, but the default per block is 48KB (configurable up to 96KB or more depending on settings). 48KB is the typical CUDA default maximum per block without explicit configuration. Wait, 3 * 32 * 128 * 4 = 3 * 16,384 = 49,152 bytes = 48.0 KiB exactly. Hmm, this is exactly at the limit. Actually, the default shared memory per block is 48KB (49,152 bytes). So we're right at the limit. But some CUDA versions/devices allow slightly more, or the compiler might add a few bytes of overhead. To be safe, I should reduce shared memory slightly. Or I can store just K and V in shared memory, and load Q directly from global memory since each warp only needs one query vector. If I keep only K and V in shared memory: - Shared memory: 2 * 32 * 128 * 4 = 32,768 bytes = 32 KB. Very safe. - Q is read directly from global memory by each warp. Actually, for better memory access, I can keep all three in shared memory if I use `cudaFuncSetAttribute` to request more shared memory. Let me just request `extern __shared__ float smem[];` and pass the size at kernel launch. For 49,152 bytes, I should be fine on H100. But actually, I don't need to store all three in shared memory. Q is only read once per warp (each warp reads one query vector). K and V are read multiple times (K is read once per key per warp, V is read once per key per output dimension computation). Wait, each warp computes scores with all keys (up to qid+1 keys). So K is accessed (qid+1) times. V is accessed (qid+1) * (head_dim/32) times. So K and V benefit more from shared memory. Q is accessed once. Let me put all three in shared memory since it fits. Actually, I'll just put Q, K, V in shared memory. 48KB should be fine. If I want to be extra safe, I'll compute exact bytes and request them. Let me also consider whether I should use float4 loads for better memory bandwidth. For 128-dim vectors, 128/4 = 32 float4 loads. This is efficient. But for simplicity, I'll start with scalar loads and optimize if needed. Now, how much speedup can this kernel give? PyTorch's default attention for this case: 1. Q @ K^T: calls cuBLAS with small batched matrices. Overhead: ~50-100us. Compute: very small. 2. Score masking and softmax: custom kernel or PyTorch ops. ~50us. 3. Softmax @ V: calls cuBLAS. Overhead: ~50-100us. Total per layer: ~200-300us of kernel time, much of which is launch overhead. My custom kernel: 1 kernel launch per layer. ~100-200us. Savings: ~100-200us per layer. Over 32 layers: ~3-6ms. If total forward is 500ms, that's ~1%. Not huge. But wait, PyTorch 2.0's `scaled_dot_product_attention` might use FlashAttention, which for this case might not be ideal due to short sequence length. The `scaled_dot_product_attention` dispatch logic might choose different backends. If the reference uses `scaled_dot_product_attention` (because transformers library might call it), the attention could be optimized already. If I use a custom kernel, I might not beat it. Hmm, let me reconsider. Is there a bigger opportunity? What if I write a kernel that fuses the ENTIRE decoder layer, or at least the FFN? The FFN has two linear layers with GELU in between. I can't beat cuBLAS on the linear layers, but I can fuse the GELU and biases. Actually, for the FFN: - Linear1: (B*S, D) @ (D, 4D) -> (B*S, 4D) - Bias add1: element-wise - GELU: element-wise - Linear2: (B*S, 4D) @ (4D, D) -> (B*S, D) - Bias add2: element-wise - Residual: element-wise PyTorch's F.linear does an efficient fused matmul+bias via cuBLAS. GELU is a separate kernel. The second linear is another cuBLAS call. Then bias add and residual are element-wise kernels. Fusing bias1+GELU+bias2+residual with the linear layers is hard. But fusing just the element-wise parts between/after linear layers saves memory bandwidth. Specifically: 1. After linear1: we can fuse bias1 + GELU into one kernel, saving one read/write of the (B*S, 4D) activation. 2. After linear2: we can fuse bias2 + residual into one kernel, saving another read/write. For 16384 * 10240 * 4 bytes = 671 MB, saving one read+write = 2 * 671MB = 1.3GB memory traffic. At 3.35 TB/s, that's ~0.4ms per layer. Over 32 layers: ~13ms. This is better than the attention optimization. And these kernels are simpler to write. Let me implement: 1. `fused_bias_gelu`: adds bias and applies GELU 2. `fused_bias_residual`: adds bias and adds residual Wait, the second one is essentially: `out = linear_out + bias + residual`. These are simple element-wise kernels that I can implement easily. Actually, `F.gelu(x + bias)` is better written as `F.gelu(F.linear(...))`. The bias add is included in the linear. So maybe I can't fuse it without writing my own linear layer, which would be slower than cuBLAS. Hmm, but what if I do: 1. `matmul1 = x @ W1^T` (using cuBLAS via `torch.matmul`) 2. `mid = fused_bias_gelu(matmul1, bias1)` (custom kernel) 3. `matmul2 = mid @ W2^T` (using cuBLAS) 4. `out = fused_bias_add_residual(matmul2, bias2, residual)` (custom kernel) This is valid! The custom kernels are simple element-wise operations, but they save memory bandwidth by fusing multiple ops. For `fused_bias_gelu`: ```cuda __global__ void fused_bias_gelu(float* out, const float* bias, int features) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = gridDim.x * blockDim.x; // or pass total_elements // Actually, we need to parallelize over all elements // For (batch*seq, 4*hidden) tensor and (4*hidden,) bias // Each thread handles one element: out[i, j] = gelu(out[i, j] + bias[j]) } ``` For `fused_bias_residual`: ```cuda __global__ void fused_bias_residual(float* out, const float* bias, const float* residual, int features) { // out[i, j] = out[i, j] + bias[j] + residual[i, j] } ``` These are very simple and will definitely save memory bandwidth. For the attention, I can still write a custom kernel, but maybe the bandwidth savings from the FFN fusions will be more impactful. Actually, I just realized: if I reimplement the model manually, I can also optimize the weight layout and use `torch.addmm` which sometimes has better performance than `F.linear`. But the biggest win might be from `torch.inference_mode()`. Let me think about how much. In PyTorch, when you run a model in training mode (`self.training = True`), even if you don't call `.backward()`, the forward pass: 1. Creates an autograd graph 2. Each operation saves tensors for backward 3. This uses extra memory and CPU overhead For a 2.7B model with batch=512, the autograd graph is HUGE. Can I optimize the matmuls? - PyTorch uses cuBLAS, which is highly optimized - Using `torch.matmul` with transposed weights is already near-optimal - One optimization:确保输入张量是连续的，内存布局友好 Another optimization: for the LM head, the weight matrix is (vocab_size, hidden_size) = (50257, 2560). This is not a multiple of 8 or 16, which means Tensor Core usage might not be optimal. But cuBLAS pads internally. Actually, one often-overlooked optimization is **weight pre-transposition**. `nn.Linear` stores weight as `(out_features, in_features)`. The forward does `x @ W^T`. cuBLAS handles transposes internally, but sometimes pre-transposing can help with memory access patterns. For the attention QKV projections, the weights are (hidden_size, hidden_size) = (2560, 2560). This is a nice square matrix. For the FFN, weights are (10240, 2560) and (2560, 10240). In my reimplementation, I'll use `torch.nn.functional.linear(x, weight, bias)` which is equivalent to `x @ weight.T + bias` and is well-optimized. So instead of manually reimplementing, I can loop through the model's original layers: ```python def forward(self, x): with torch.inference_mode(): inputs_embeds = self.model.transformer.wte(x) position_ids = torch.arange(0, x.size(1), dtype=torch.long, device=x.device).unsqueeze(0) hidden = inputs_embeds + self.model.transformer.wpe(position_ids) for block in self.model.transformer.h: # Layer 1 normed = block.ln_1(hidden) q = block.attn.attention.q_proj(normed) k = block.attn.attention.k_proj(normed) v = block.attn.attention.v_proj(normed) # Reshape and call custom attention q = q.view(...).transpose(...) k = k.view(...).transpose(...) v = v.view(...).transpose(...) attn_out = custom_attention(q, k, v) attn_out = attn_out.transpose(...).contiguous().view(...) attn_out = block.attn.attention.out_proj(attn_out) hidden = hidden + attn_out # Layer 2 normed = block.ln_2(hidden) mlp_out = block.mlp.c_fc(normed) mlp_out = block.mlp.act(mlp_out) mlp_out = block.mlp.c_proj(mlp_out) hidden = hidden + mlp_out hidden = self.model.transformer.ln_f(hidden) logits = self.model.lm_head(hidden) return logits ``` This is great! Let me write a version without shared memory to avoid any limit issues: ```cuda __global__ void short_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int H, int S, int D ) { int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; if (warp_id >= S) return; int qid = warp_id; const float* q_vec = q + ((b * H + h) * S + qid) * D; float scores[32]; float max_score = -1e30f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { const float* k_vec = k + ((b * H + h) * S + kid) * D; float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = lane_id; d < D; d += 32) { dot += q_vec[d] * k_vec[d]; } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { float scale = rsqrtf((float)D); scores[kid] = dot * scale; if (scores[kid] > max_score) max_score = scores[kid]; } } max_score = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_score, 0); float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { if (lane_id == 0) { scores[kid] = expf(scores[kid] - max_score); exp_sum += scores[kid]; } } exp_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, exp_sum, 0); for (int d = lane_id; d < D; d += 32) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { float score = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, scores[kid] / exp_sum, 0); const float* v_vec = v + ((b * H + h) * S + kid) * D; sum += score * v_vec[d]; } out[((b * H + h) * S + qid) * D + d] = sum; } } ``` Wait, there's an issue with `scores` array. This works because lane 0 computed and stored the score in its `scores[kid]`. All other lanes also have `scores[kid]`, but the value is uninitialized (except they copied it via shfl). Here's the revised kernel: ```cuda __global__ void short_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int H, int S, int D ) { int bh = blockIdx.x; Since `lane_id == 0` executes the softmax loop and other lanes compute the weighted sum, there is a potential race condition within the warp. In CUDA, within a warp, if lane 0 writes to shared memory and lane 1 reads from it, the read might happen before the write without explicit synchronization. But actually, in CUDA's SIMT model, threads in a warp execute in lockstep (except for divergent branches). The divergent branch where lane_id==0 executes the softmax loop while other lanes skip it means the other lanes are inactive during that loop. After the loop, all lanes converge and continue together. At that point, lane 0's writes to shared memory should be visible to all threads in the warp. So I'll write the kernel without the early return: ```cuda __global__ void short_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int H, int S, int D ) { int bh = blockIdx.x; Actually, let me look at this from a compiler's perspective. `scores` is an array of 32 floats. Lane 0 writes to it. When we do `__shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, scores[kid], 0)`, lane 0 needs to have `scores[kid]` in a register. The compiler might or might not keep the entire array in registers. Bad idea. What if I precompute ALL dot products in the first pass and store normalized scores in a float array in registers: ```cuda float norm_scores[32]; // one per thread // Only lane 0 uses the full array. But we declare it in all threads. ``` Actually, in C/CUDA, each thread has its own `norm_scores[32]`. This is 128 bytes per thread. For 1024 threads, 128KB total. The compiler might spill some of it. Let me just use the shared memory approach with `__syncthreads()`. It's cleaner, and since all threads in the block always reach `__syncthreads()` (no early returns when block=1024, S=32), it's safe. So the final kernel design is: - Block: 1024 threads (32 warps) - Grid: B * H blocks - Shared memory: 32 * 32 * sizeof(float) = 4KB (for scores) - Each warp handles one query position (qid = warp_id) - Each thread in a warp handles head_dim elements with stride 32 Let me refine the shared memory kernel: ```cuda __global__ void short_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int H, int S, int D ) { int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; int qid = warp_id; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* scores_smem = smem + warp_id * S; // Each warp gets S floats const float* q_vec = q + ((b * H + h) * S + qid) * D; float scale = rsqrtf((float)D); // Compute dot products float max_score = -1e30f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { const float* k_vec = k + ((b * H + h) * S + kid) * D; float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = lane_id; d < D; d += 32) { dot += q_vec[d] * k_vec[d]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { float score = dot * scale; scores_smem[kid] = score; if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } } max_score = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_score, 0); // Softmax if (lane_id == 0) { float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { float e = expf(scores_smem[kid] - max_score); scores_smem[kid] = e; exp_sum += e; } for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { scores_smem[kid] /= exp_sum; } } __syncwarp(); // Ensure lane 0's writes are visible to other lanes in this warp // Weighted sum of V for (int d = lane_id; d < D; d += 32) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { float score = scores_smem[kid]; const float* v_vec = v + ((b * H + h) * S + kid) * D; sum += score * v_vec[d]; } out[((b * H + h) * S + qid) * D + d] = sum; } } ``` Wait, `__syncwarp()` is a good choice here! It synchronizes all threads in the warp, ensuring lane 0's writes to shared memory are visible to other lanes in the same warp. Since we're using `extern __shared__`, but each warp only accesses its own section, `__syncwarp()` is sufficient and avoids the need for `__syncthreads()`. But `__syncwarp()` might not be necessary because in the SIMT model, all threads in a warp execute together, and after the divergent branch (lane_id==0) completes, the warp reconverges and the writes should be visible. However, to be safe and avoid memory ordering issues, I'll use `__syncwarp()`. Actually, looking at CUDA documentation: within a warp, there are no memory ordering issues for shared memory between threads as long as there's no divergence. With divergence (lane_id==0), the threads reconverge before executing the next instruction. Shared memory writes by lane 0 should be visible to other lanes after reconvergence. But `__syncwarp()` is a safer bet. Now, there is one remaining issue: the `extern __shared__` memory declaration. For `load_inline`, I need to make sure the kernel is valid C++. Also, I need to handle the template properly. The kernel takes `int` parameters which are passed from Python. For the Python interface: ```python def forward(self, x): # ... attn_out = custom_ops.short_causal_attention(q, k, v) # ... ``` where `q, k, v` are tensors of shape `(batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim)`. Actually, `__syncwarp()` only synchronizes threads within a warp that execute it. If a warp has `warp_id >= S`, all 32 threads in that warp return, so none of them call `__syncwarp()`. That's fine because the warp is completely inactive. The problem is with `__syncthreads()`, which requires ALL threads in the block to reach it. But I'm using `__syncwarp()`, not `__syncthreads()`. In my custom kernel, I use `int` for indexing. With B=512, H=20, S=32, D=128: - Total elements per tensor: 512 * 20 * 32 * 128 = 41,943,040 - Index calculation: `((b * H + h) * S + qid) * D` - For b=511, h=19, qid=31: ((511*20 + 19)*32 + 31)*128 = ((10220 + 19)*32 + 31)*128 = (10239*32 + 31)*128 = (327,648 + 31)*128 = 41,790,912 This fits in a 32-bit signed integer (max ~2 billion). One issue: `q`, `k`, `v` might not be contiguous. If they come from `.transpose(1, 2)`, they ARE contiguous if `.contiguous()` was called. In my code: ```python q = q.view(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1, 2) ``` Wait, `.view(...)` might fail if the tensor isn't contiguous. And `.transpose(1, 2)` creates a non-contiguous tensor. So when I pass `q` to the custom kernel, it might not be contiguous in memory! This is a critical issue. My kernel assumes a specific memory layout: elements within a head are contiguous, and the sequence dimension has a stride of head_dim. But after transpose, the layout is: - Stride for dim 0 (batch): seq_len * num_heads * head_dim - Stride for dim 1 (heads): head_dim - Stride for dim 2 (seq): num_heads * head_dim - Stride for dim 3 (head_dim): 1 Wait, let me compute strides after `view(batch, seq, heads, dim).transpose(1, 2)`: Original after view: (batch, seq, heads, dim) Strides: [seq*heads*dim, heads*dim, dim, 1] After transpose(1, 2): (batch, heads, seq, dim) Strides: [seq*heads*dim, dim, heads*dim, 1] So in the transposed tensor, moving along the sequence dimension (dim 2) has stride `heads*dim`, and moving along the head dimension (dim 1) has stride `dim`. My kernel computes offsets as: ```cuda const float* q_vec = q + (((b * H + h) * S) + qid) * D; ``` This assumes the memory layout where `(b, h, s, d)` maps to index `((b * H + h) * S + s) * D + d`. This is a contiguous layout with strides [H*S*D, S*D, D, 1]. But after transpose, the strides are [S*H*D, D, H*D, 1]. So my formula is WRONG! Specifically, to access element (b, h, s, d), the correct offset is: `b * stride[0] + h * stride[1] + s * stride[2] + d` = `b * (S*H*D) + h * D + s * (H*D) + d` My formula assumes `stride[1] = S*D` and `stride[2] = D`, but after transpose, `stride[1] = D` and `stride[2] = H*D`. This is a critical bug! I need to either: 1. Call `.contiguous()` after transpose before passing to the kernel 2. Or modify the kernel to use the correct strides 3. Or don't transpose, and instead adapt the kernel Option 1 is easiest: `q = q.view(...).transpose(1, 2).contiguous()`. This ensures the memory layout matches my formula. The `.contiguous()` call might involve a memory copy, but it's a one-time cost per layer. Similarly for `k` and `v`. After the custom kernel returns `attn_out`, I need to call `.transpose(1, 2).contiguous().view(...)` to reshape back. Wait, actually, in the original `GPTNeoSelfAttention`, the Q, K, V are split and transposed but might not be made contiguous explicitly. PyTorch's matmul handles non-contiguous tensors fine. In my implementation, calling `.contiguous()` on Q, K, V is an extra memory copy. For 512*20*32*128*4 bytes = ~160 MB per tensor, and 3 tensors per layer, that's 480 MB of memory copies per layer. This bandwidth cost could offset the benefit of the custom kernel! For 32 layers: 32 * 480 MB = 15.36 GB of copy traffic. At 3.35 TB/s, that's ~4.6 ms. Hmm, for 32 layers, the total attention time might be ~20ms, so 4.6ms extra is significant but not prohibitive. However, if I modify the kernel to accept strides, I can avoid the copies. Let me modify the kernel to accept stride information: ```python torch::Tensor short_causal_attention(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { // q is (B, H, S, D) but not necessarily contiguous auto out = torch::empty_like(q); // empty_like preserves strides! // But my kernel writes as if contiguous, which is WRONG if out has non-unit strides ``` Actually, `torch::empty_like(q)` preserves the strides of `q`. If `q` is non-contiguous, `out` will also be non-contiguous, with the same strides. My kernel would write to the wrong locations. I should make `out` contiguous: `auto out = torch::empty({B, H, S, D}, q.options());`. This creates a contiguous tensor. But then in Python, I need to make sure the output shape matches what I expect. Actually, easier fix: in Python, call `.contiguous()` on q, k, v before passing them: ```python q = q.transpose(1, 2).contiguous() k = k.transpose(1, 2).contiguous() v = v.transpose(1, 2).contiguous() attn_out = self.custom_ops.short_causal_attention(q, k, v) attn_out = attn_out.transpose(1, 2).contiguous().view(batch_size, seq_len, hidden_size) ``` Wait, after the kernel, `attn_out` is `(B, H, S, D)` contiguous. I need to transpose to `(B, S, H, D)` and then reshape to `(B, S, H*D)`. So `attn_out.transpose(1, 2).contiguous().view(...)` creates another copy. Total copies per layer: 1. q contiguous: 160 MB 2. k contiguous: 160 MB 3. v contiguous: 160 MB 4. attn_out transpose + contiguous: 160 MB Total: 640 MB per layer. For 32 layers: 20.48 GB. At 3.35 TB/s: ~6.1 ms. Is this worth it? The attention computation itself is at most ~10ms for all layers (being generous). So the copy overhead might dominate. Alternative: write the kernel to handle non-contiguous tensors. We can pass strides to the kernel and compute offsets correctly. Here's a revised kernel that accepts strides: ```cuda __global__ void short_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int H, int S, int D, long q_stride_b, long q_stride_h, long q_stride_s, long k_stride_b, long k_stride_h, long k_stride_s, long v_stride_b, long v_stride_h, long v_stride_s, long o_stride_b, long o_stride_h, long o_stride_s ) { int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* scores_smem = smem + warp_id * S; if (warp_id >= S) return; int qid = warp_id; const float* q_vec = q + b * q_stride_b + h * q_stride_h + qid * q_stride_s; float scale = rsqrtf((float)D); float max_score = -1e30f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { const float* k_vec = k + b * k_stride_b + h * k_stride_h + kid * k_stride_s; float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = lane_id; d < D; d += 32) { dot += q_vec[d] * k_vec[d]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { float score = dot * scale; scores_smem[kid] = score; if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } } max_score = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_score, 0); if (lane_id == 0) { float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { float e = expf(scores_smem[kid] - max_score); scores_smem[kid] = e; exp_sum += e; } for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { scores_smem[kid] /= exp_sum; } } __syncwarp(); for (int d = lane_id; d < D; d += 32) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { float score = scores_smem[kid]; const float* v_vec = v + b * v_stride_b + h * v_stride_h + kid * v_stride_s; sum += score * v_vec[d]; } out[b * o_stride_b + h * o_stride_h + qid * o_stride_s + d] = sum; } } ``` This is much better! It handles arbitrary strides. I get the strides from PyTorch tensors using `q.stride(0)`, `q.stride(1)`, `q.stride(2)`. But wait, after splitting and transposing in the standard way: - q.stride(0) = seq_len * num_heads * head_dim - q.stride(1) = head_dim - q.stride(2) = num_heads * head_dim And since head_dim = D, q.stride(2) = H * D. My formula `b * q_stride_b + h * q_stride_h + qid * q_stride_s + d` correctly accesses element (b, h, qid, d). The output `out` is created with `torch::empty_like(q)`. This preserves the same strides as `q`. But wait - we might want `out` to have the same strides as `q`. Since `q`, `k`, `v` all have the same shape and were produced similarly, they should have the same strides. But to be safe, I'll pass the strides of `out` explicitly. Actually, using `empty_like(q)` is fine if we pass `out.stride(0)`, `out.stride(1)`, `out.stride(2)`. One more thing: `q_vec[d]` assumes stride of the last dimension is 1. Let me verify: `q_vec = q + b*q_stride_b + h*q_stride_h + qid*q_stride_s`. Then `q_vec[d]` accesses `d` elements with stride 1. This is correct if the last dimension has stride 1. For tensors created by `.transpose(1, 2)` on a view, the last dimension is `head_dim` which indeed has stride 1. Let me verify with the view + transpose: `tensor.view(B, S, H, D).transpose(1, 2)` Result shape: (B, H, S, D) Strides: - stride[0] = S * H * D (since we moved by S positions in original, each of size H*D) - stride[1] = D (since we moved by 1 position in dim 1 of result, which corresponds to dim 2 of original with stride D) - stride[2] = H * D (since we moved by 1 position in dim 2 of result, which corresponds to dim 1 of original with stride H*D) - stride[3] = 1 (last dim) Yes! stride[3] = 1. So `q_vec[d]` is correct. Similarly for `k_vec[d]`, `v_vec[d]`, and `out[... + d]`. Great, this kernel handles non-contiguous tensors correctly without copies! Now, the Python wrapper: ```python def short_causal_attention(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v): int B = q.size(0); int H = q.size(1); int S = q.size(2); int D = q.size(3); auto out = torch::empty_like(q); int threads = 1024; int blocks = B * H; size_t shared_mem = 32 * S * sizeof(float); // 32 warps, S scores each short_causal_attention_kernel<<<blocks, threads, shared_mem>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, H, S, D, q.stride(0), q.stride(1), q.stride(2), k.stride(0), k.stride(1), k.stride(2), v.stride(0), v.stride(1), v.stride(2), out.stride(0), out.stride(1), out.stride(2)); return out; } ``` Wait, `q.stride(0)` in C++ returns an `int64_t`. I need to make sure the function signature accepts `long` or `int64_t`. For `long`, on some platforms it's 32-bit. Safer to use `int64_t` or `long long`. But let me check what `torch::Tensor::stride()` returns. It returns `int64_t`. So I'll use `int64_t` in the kernel signature. ```cuda __global__ void short_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int H, int S, int D, int64_t q_stride_b, int64_t q_stride_h, int64_t q_stride_s, int64_t k_stride_b, int64_t k_stride_h, int64_t k_stride_s, int64_t v_stride_b, int64_t v_stride_h, int64_t v_stride_s, int64_t o_stride_b, int64_t o_stride_h, int64_t o_stride_s ) { ... } ``` This is robust. Now, one more important detail: `if (warp_id >= S) return;` causes threads in warps with `warp_id >= S` to return early. But `__syncwarp()` is only called by warps that don't return early. Since threads that return early don't call `__syncwarp()`, and each `__syncwarp()` only synchronizes its own warp, there is no problem. Each active warp calls `__syncwarp()` for its own threads. Wait, but `__syncwarp()` is only called by active warps. The warps with `warp_id >= S` don't call it. Since each warp is independent, this is fine. But what about the allocation of shared memory? With `extern __shared__ float smem[]`, and `scores_smem = smem + warp_id * S`, a warp with `warp_id=31` and `S=32` would access `smem + 31*32 = smem + 992`. The shared memory size is `32 * S * sizeof(float) = 1024 * 4 = 4096` bytes. `scores_smem[0..31]` for warp 31 is at indices `992..1023` of the float array. This is within bounds since `32*32=1024` elements, indices 0..1023. Good. If `S=32` and block size is 1024, `warp_id` ranges from 0 to 31. All valid. No issues. Now, I'm concerned about whether the kernel will be faster than the original attention. Let me think about launch overhead vs saved computation. For global layers (16 layers), each layer launches my custom kernel once. The kernel has: - Grid: B * H = 512 * 20 = 10,240 blocks - Threads per block: 1024 - Shared memory: 4KB On H100, each SM can handle multiple blocks. With 10,240 blocks and ~132 SMs on H100 (actually, H100 has 132 or 144 SMs depending on the variant), each SM gets ~77 blocks. With 1024 threads per block and good occupancy, this should run efficiently. The actual computation per block is very small: 32 warps, each handling one query position. Most of the time is spent in the dot products and weighted sums. For the dot product: 128 multiplications and 128 additions per key, and each warp processes up to 32 keys. Total FLOPs per warp: ~32 * 128 * 2 = ~8K FLOPs. With 10,240 warps total, total FLOPs: ~82M FLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS, this is ~1.2 microseconds of pure compute. But memory bandwidth and kernel overhead dominate. The kernel should run in maybe 50-100 microseconds per layer. For 16 layers (global), that's ~1ms. For the original attention using `torch.matmul`, it might be similar or slightly slower due to multiple kernel launches. So the custom kernel might save a millisecond or two over the global layers. Actually, looking at `GPTNeoAttention.forward` more carefully: ```python def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask=None, layer_past=None, head_mask=None, use_cache=False, output_attentions=False, cache_position=None): # Generate causal mask if needed attn_past = layer_past ... attn_outputs = self.attention(hidden_states, attention_mask=attention_mask, ...) ... return attn_output, present ``` For `GPTNeoSelfAttention.forward`: ```python def forward(self, hidden_states, attention_mask=None, layer_past=None, ...): query = self.q_proj(hidden_states) key = self.k_proj(hidden_states) value = self.v_proj(hidden_states) query = self._split_heads(query) key = self._split_heads(key) value = self._split_heads(value) if layer_past is not None: past_key = layer_past[0] past_value = layer_past[1] key = torch.cat((past_key, key), dim=-2) value = torch.cat((past_value, value), dim=-2) attn_weights = torch.matmul(query, key.transpose(-1, -2)) attn_weights = attn_weights / math.sqrt(self.head_dim) if attention_mask is not None: attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1) attn_weights = self.attn_dropout(attn_weights) attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_weights, value) attn_output = self._merge_heads(attn_output) attn_output = self.out_proj(attn_output) attn_output = self.resid_dropout(attn_output) return attn_output, present ``` So when `layer_past=None` and `attention_mask=None`, and we're in eval mode (dropout disabled), the computation is: 1. QKV projections 2. Split heads 3. Q @ K^T / sqrt(d) 4. Softmax 5. @ V 6. Merge heads 7. Out proj My custom kernel implements steps 3-5 (and includes the split/merge by taking the right shapes). """ cuda_source = """ __global__ void short_causal_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int H, int S, int D, int64_t q_stride_b, int64_t q_stride_h, int64_t q_stride_s, int64_t k_stride_b, int64_t k_stride_h, int64_t k_stride_s, int64_t v_stride_b, int64_t v_stride_h, int64_t v_stride_s, int64_t o_stride_b, int64_t o_stride_h, int64_t o_stride_s ) { int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* scores_smem = smem + warp_id * S; if (warp_id >= S) return; int qid = warp_id; const float* q_vec = q + b * q_stride_b + h * q_stride_h + qid * q_stride_s; float scale = rsqrtf((float)D); float max_score = -1e30f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { const float* k_vec = k + b * k_stride_b + h * k_stride_h + kid * k_stride_s; float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = lane_id; d < D; d += 32) { dot += q_vec[d] * k_vec[d]; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { float score = dot * scale; scores_smem[kid] = score; if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } } max_score = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_score, 0); if (lane_id == 0) { float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { float e = expf(scores_smem[kid] - max_score); scores_smem[kid] = e; exp_sum += e; } for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { scores_smem[kid] /= exp_sum; } } __syncwarp(); for (int d = lane_id; d < D; d += 32) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { float score = scores_smem[kid]; const float* v_vec = v + b * v_stride_b + h * v_stride_h + kid * v_stride_s; sum += score * v_vec[d]; } float* o_vec = out + b * o_stride_b + h * o_stride_h + qid * o_stride_s; o_vec[d] = sum; } } torch::Tensor short_causal_attention(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { int B = q.size(0); int H = q.size(1); int S = q.size(2); int D = q.size(3); auto out = torch::empty_like(q); int threads = 1024; int blocks = B * H; size_t shared_mem = 32 * S * sizeof(float); short_causal_attention_kernel<<<blocks, threads, shared_mem>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, H, S, D, q.stride(0), q.stride(1), q.stride(2), k.stride(0), k.stride(1), k.stride(2), v.stride(0), v.stride(1), v.stride(2), out.stride(0), out.stride(1), out.stride(2)); return out; } """ ``` Wait, there's an issue. `out.stride(0)` in C++ returns `int64_t`, but the variable names in the signature are `o_stride_b`, `o_stride_h`, `o_stride_s`. When I compute `out + b * o_stride_b + h * o_stride_h + qid * o_stride_s`, the result is a `float*` pointer, and `o_vec[d]` access is correct if `o_stride_s` represents the stride between sequence positions. Wait, for the output tensor of shape (B, H, S, D), `out.stride(2)` is the stride between elements that differ by 1 in dimension 2 (sequence). That is, moving from position s to s+1 in dimension 2 requires skipping `out.stride(2)` elements. But in my pointer arithmetic, `out + b * o_stride_b + h * o_stride_h + qid * o_stride_s` gives the start of the (b, h, qid, :) slice. Then `o_vec[d]` accesses `d` elements forward with stride 1. This is correct IF `out.stride(3) == 1`. For the output created with `torch::empty_like(q)`, it will have the same strides as `q`. And the last dimension of `q` has stride 1, so the output's last dimension also has stride 1. So `o_vec[d]` is correct. Similarly, `q_vec[d]`, `k_vec[d]`, `v_vec[d]` are correct because the last dimension of these tensors has stride 1. Wait, let me verify: `q` is shape (B, H, S, D) with strides [S*H*D, D, H*D, 1]. The last dimension (D) has stride 1. Yes. So `q_vec[d]` moves `d` elements forward, which correctly accesses the d-th element of the head_dim. Good. In the output write: `out + b * o_stride_b + h * o_stride_h + qid * o_stride_s + d`. This computes the offset correctly. One subtle issue: `o_stride_s` is `out.stride(2)`, which for our transposed tensor is `H*D`. So `out + qid * o_stride_s` moves by `qid * H * D` elements. This correctly skips qid sequence positions. Good. Now... wait. `o_vec = out + b * o_stride_b + h * o_stride_h + qid * o_stride_s`. Then `o_vec[d] = sum;`. This writes to the d-th element. Since the stride of the last dimension is 1, this is correct. But there's a potential issue: `out` is a `float*`, and `o_stride_b`, `o_stride_h`, `o_stride_s` are `int64_t`. When we do arithmetic on `float*`, adding an integer `N` moves by `N * sizeof(float)` bytes. But our strides are in terms of number of elements (since they come from PyTorch's `.stride()` which is in elements). Wait, in PyTorch C++ API, `tensor.stride(i)` returns the number of elements to skip, not bytes. So `q_stride_b = q.stride(0)` is the number of elements between batch items. When we do `q + b * q_stride_b`, pointer arithmetic on `float*` multiplies `q_stride_b` by `sizeof(float)` automatically. So this is correct. Great. Now, one more potential issue: `__syncwarp()`. When a warp diverges (if `lane_id == 0` does different work), `__syncwarp()` is needed to reconverge before the non-lane-0 threads read shared memory. But actually, after the `if (lane_id == 0)` block, the warp is already reconverged by the time we reach `__syncwarp()`. However, there's a memory ordering issue: lane 0 writes to `scores_smem`, and other lanes read from it. On modern GPUs, shared memory writes from threads in the same warp are visible to other threads in the same warp without explicit synchronization, IF there are no other memory operations that could reorder. Actually, CUDA documentation says: "Note that threads within a warp are implicitly synchronized on SI+ (Maxwell and later architectures), but explicit synchronization is still recommended for correctness." On newer GPUs, `__syncwarp()` is very fast. I'll keep it for safety. But actually, looking at my code again, `__syncwarp()` is only executed by threads in warps where `warp_id < S`. Threads in warps where `warp_id >= S` returned early and don't execute `__syncwarp()`. Since each `__syncwarp()` only synchronizes the 32 threads in the calling warp, and a warp either has all threads reach `__syncwarp()` or none of them (due to the early return), there's no deadlock. Wait, the early return is at the warp level: `if (warp_id >= S) return;`. All 32 threads in a warp have the same `warp_id`, so they ALL return or ALL continue. There's no divergence at the `return` statement within a warp. So this is perfectly safe! Good. Now, one more thought about the block size. I set `threads = 1024`. With `S=32`, only 32 warps out of 32 are active. If `S < 32`, some warps would be inactive. But for our case, it's exactly right. What if I set `threads = 32 * S`? attn_out.transpose(1, 2).contiguous().view(batch_size, seq_len, self.config.hidden_size) attn_out = block.attn.attention.out_proj(attn_out) else: # Local attention - use original module attn_out = For the attention specifically, the reference might use TF32 for Q@K^T and AV, while my kernel uses fp32. This is the only place where precision differs. Will this cause a >1e-4 error? Let's think: Q and K are projected hidden states, typically with std ~0.02. Dot product of 128 elements: expected magnitude ~0.02 * 0.02 * 128 = 0.05. TF32 error might be ~1e-4 absolute. After softmax, the error gets smoothed out. The final attention output is a weighted sum of values, each ~0.02-0.1. The total error in the output might be ~1e-5 to 1e-4. I'll tentatively assume it's within tolerance. If not, I can modify the kernel to use TF32... which is practically impossible without changing the whole approach. Or I can just not use the custom kernel for attention and instead write a kernel that does something less precision-sensitive. If the attention kernel fails correctness due to precision, I might need a backup plan. Backup plan: write a kernel that fuses element-wise operations instead of attention. For example, a kernel that fuses `bias_add + GELU` for the MLP. Actually, let me think about what element-wise kernel would be most impactful and easiest to get correct. For the MLP: ```python mlp_out = block.mlp.c_fc(normed) mlp_out = block.mlp.act(mlp_out) mlp_out = block.mlp.c_proj(mlp_out) ``` I can't fuse the linear layers with a custom kernel efficiently. But I CAN fuse the `c_fc` output + activation + `c_proj`... no, that doesn't work because `c_proj` is a linear layer. Wait, what about fusing the final bias + residual in each sublayer? After `out_proj` in attention: `attn_out = block.attn.attention.out_proj(attn_output)` Then: `hidden = hidden + attn_out` If `out_proj` has bias, this is: ```python attn_out = matmul(attn_input, W_out.T) + b_out hidden = hidden + attn_out ``` This is two element-wise passes over the tensor: one for bias add, one for residual add. A custom kernel could do: `hidden = hidden + matmul_result + bias`. But we can't fuse the matmul. What about just fusing `bias_add + residual_add`? ```cuda __global__ void fused_add_bias_residual(float* hidden, const float* linear_out, const float* bias, int features) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = gridDim.x * blockDim.x; // Need to parallelize over all elements } ``` Wait, this is harder to write efficiently for arbitrary shapes. But for known shapes, we can do it. Actually, for `hidden` of shape `(batch*seq, hidden_size)`, and `linear_out` and `bias` of compatible shapes, a simple element-wise kernel is easy. But the speedup from fusing two adds is minimal: saves one read/write pass over the activation. For 16384 * 2560 * 4 bytes = 168 MB, saving one read+write = 336 MB of memory traffic. At 3.35 TB/s, that's 0.1 ms per layer. Over 64 sublayers (attention + MLP for 32 layers): 6.4 ms. For a total runtime of 500ms, that's ~1.3%. So element-wise fusions are less impactful than I thought. But if my attention kernel fails correctness, I might need to rely on these smaller wins combined with `inference_mode()`. Actually, maybe I should just try the attention kernel first. If it works, great. If not, I'll switch to a simpler kernel and lean more on `inference_mode()` and `eval()`. Wait, looking at `GPTNeoSelfAttention`: ```python class GPTNeoSelfAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config, attention_type): super().__init__() max_positions = config.max_position_embeddings self.register_buffer( "bias", torch.tril(torch.ones((max_positions, max_positions), dtype=torch.bool)).view( 1, 1, max_positions, max_positions ), persistent=False, ) self.register_buffer("masked_bias", torch.tensor(-1e9), persistent=False) self.attention_type = attention_type self.num_heads = config.num_heads self.head_dim = config.hidden_size // config.num_heads self.split_size = config.hidden_size self.hidden_size = config.hidden_size if self.attention_type == "global": self.q_proj = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, config.hidden_size, bias=True) self.k_proj = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, self.head_dim, bias=False) self.v_proj = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, config.hidden_size, bias=True) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, config.hidden_size, bias=True) ``` Wait! `self.k_proj` has output size `self.head_dim`, not `config.hidden_size`! Actually wait, I think I found a potential problem with our kernel. The kernel uses: ```cpp int threads = 1024; int blocks = B * H; ``` And inside the kernel, we have `int warp_id = tid / 32;`. With 1024 threads, warp_id ranges from 0 to 31. But S = 32. So warp_ids 0..31 correspond to query positions 0..31. That's fine. But what about `lane_id`? `lane_id = tid % 32`, ranges from 0 to 31. And D = 128. So for computing dot products, `for (int d = lane_id; d < D; d += 32)` covers d = 0, 32, 64, 96. Each warp computes one row of the attention. Wait, `d += 32` for `lane_id = 0` gives d = 0, 32, 64, 96. For `lane_id = 1` gives d = 1, 33, 65, 97. ... Each lane processes 4 elements. The dot product is accumulated across 4 elements, then reduced with shfl_down. That looks correct. And for the V weighted sum: ```cpp for (int d = lane_id; d < D; d += 32) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int kid = 0; kid <= qid; kid++) { float score = scores_smem[kid]; const float* v_vec = v + b * v_stride_b + h * v_stride_h + kid * v_stride_s; sum += score * v_vec[d]; } float* o_vec = out + b * o_stride_b + h * o_stride_h + qid * o_stride_s; o_vec[d] = sum; } ``` The `v + b * v_stride_b + h * v_stride_h + kid * v_stride_s` points to the start of the kid-th token's value vector for head h, batch b. And `v_vec[d]` accesses element d. This is correct if V strides are for the tensor shape (B, H, S, D). But one issue: we never check if the output strides correspond to shape (B, H, S, D). However, after `transpose(1, 2)` the tensor should be contiguous or at least have proper strides for this to work. Actually, we call `.transpose(1, 2)` but don't call `.contiguous()`. After transpose, the tensor might not be contiguous, but the strides should correctly reflect the transposed shape. Our kernel uses explicit strides, so it should handle non-contiguous tensors. Wait, but we only transpose q, k, v. And then pass them to the kernel. After `view` and `transpose`, the tensors might have weird strides but they should be valid. Actually, for `q.view(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1, 2)`, the stride becomes something like: - stride[0] = original stride[0] (seq_len * hidden_size) - stride[1] = head_dim (was original stride[2] after view) - stride[2] = hidden_size (was original stride[1] after view) - stride[3] = 1 Wait, let me think. The original q has shape (B, S, H*D) with stride (S*H*D, H*D, 1). After `view(B, S, H, D)`, stride is (S*H*D, H*D, D, 1). After `transpose(1, 2)`, stride becomes (S*H*D, D, H*D, 1). So for tensor `q` with shape (B, H, S, D) after transpose: - q.stride(0) = S*H*D - q.stride(1) = D - q.stride(2) = H*D - q.stride(3) = 1 In the kernel, we pass `q.stride(0), q.stride(1), q.stride(2)` as q_stride_b, q_stride_h, q_stride_s. - q_stride_b = S*H*D (correct, distance between batch elements) - q_stride_h = D (correct, distance between head elements) - q_stride_s = H*D (correct, distance between sequence elements) Then inside the kernel: ```cpp const float* q_vec = q + b * q_stride_b + h * q_stride_h + qid * q_stride_s; ``` This gives the address of the qid-th token for head h, batch b. Good. ```cpp const float* k_vec = k + b * k_stride_b + h * k_stride_h + kid * k_stride_s; ``` Same logic for k. ```cpp const float* v_vec = v + b * v_stride_b + h * v_stride_h + kid * v_stride_s; sum += score * v_vec[d]; ``` And: ```cpp float* o_vec = out + b * o_stride_b + h * o_stride_h + qid * o_stride_s; o_vec[d] = sum; ``` This all looks correct! Looking at the `GPTNeoSelfAttention._attn` method: ```python def _attn(self, query, key, value, attention_mask=None, head_mask=None): # Keep the attention weights computation in fp32 to avoid overflow issues query = query.to(torch.float32) key = key.to(torch.float32) attn_weights = torch.matmul(query, key.transpose(-1, -2)) attn_weights = torch.where(self.bias, attn_weights, self.masked_bias.to(attn_weights.dtype)) if attention_mask is not None: attn_weights = attn_weights + attention_mask attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1) attn_weights = attn_weights.to(value.dtype) attn_weights = nn.functional.dropout(attn_weights, p=self.attention_dropout, training=self.training) attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_weights, value) return attn_output, attn_weights ``` Note: `self.bias` is a buffer that contains the causal mask (lower triangular of ones). `self.masked_bias` is `-1e9`. Our kernel computes causal attention by only computing dot products for `kid <= qid` and treating the rest as zero in softmax (they don't contribute). This is mathematically equivalent to masking with `-inf` before softmax. However, our kernel uses the raw float values directly. The reference uses fp32 computation even if the model is in fp16. But the model is in fp32 by default, so this shouldn't matter. Another thing: the reference computes Q@K^T as a full SxS matrix, then masks, then softmax. Our kernel only computes causal part. They should be numerically equivalent (ignoring floating point differences from different reduction orders). But wait - the reference might use a different scale factor. In `GPTNeoSelfAttention._attn`, the matmul result is NOT scaled by 1/sqrt(D). Let me check again... Actually, yes! In GPT-Neo, the attention weights are: ```python attn_weights = torch.matmul(query, key.transpose(-1, -2)) attn_weights = torch.where(self.bias, attn_weights, self.masked_bias.to(attn_weights.dtype)) ``` There is no explicit scaling by `1/sqrt(head_dim)` in `_attn`.
